## 

## 

## 

## **Abstract**

**Background:** Recruitment agencies in South Africa face significant operational challenges due to the manual processing of high-volume CVs. Tumaini Consulting, a majority Black-owned recruitment agency based in Cape Town, processes over 25,000 CVs annually but relies on manual screening where recruiters spend 5-15 hours reviewing 500-3,000 CVs per role. This manual process leads to recruiter fatigue, inconsistent shortlisting, delayed client deliverables, and missed talent.

**Objective:** This research aimed to design, develop, and evaluate an AI-powered recruitment platform that automates CV screening and candidate matching using Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG), vector databases, and semantic search, while following Domain-Driven Design (DDD) principles and microservices architecture.

**Methodology:** The project followed Agile Scrum methodology with eight 2-week sprints. The system was architected as five microservices (Identity, CV, Vector, Matching, Job) with a React frontend and Kong API gateway. Domain-Driven Design guided the strategic design with five bounded contexts. The AI pipeline used sentence-transformers (all-MiniLM-L6-v2) for embeddings, Qdrant as the vector database, and GPT-4o-mini with Llama 3 fallback for RAG-based candidate scoring.

**Results:** The delivered platform successfully reduced candidate screening time from 5-15 hours to under 1.5 hours per role (90% reduction). Time-to-shortlist decreased from 8-12 days to ≤4 days (67% reduction). The 3-day candidate response standard improved from 70% to 96% adherence. Semantic search achieved \<200ms latency at 20,000 vectors with \>0.95 recall. The system processed 15,000+ historical CVs into searchable vectors. All 200 story points were completed across 8 sprints with 100% acceptance criteria pass rate.

**Conclusion:** This research demonstrates that AI-powered recruitment platforms using RAG and vector databases can significantly reduce manual screening time while improving consistency and transparency. The DDD and microservices approach proved effective for managing domain complexity. The platform honours Tumaini's name by delivering faster, fairer, and more transparent recruitment to candidates and clients across South Africa.

**Keywords:** Artificial Intelligence, Recruitment, Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG), Vector Database, Domain-Driven Design, Microservices, Semantic Search, Natural Language Processing, South Africa, POPIA.

---

# 

# **Chapter 1: Introduction**

## **1.1 Background of the Study**

### **1.1.1 The Recruitment Industry Challenge**

The global recruitment industry has undergone significant transformation over the past decade, driven by technological advancements and changing workforce dynamics. According to Statista (2025), the global recruitment market was valued at approximately $800 billion in 2025, with digital recruitment platforms growing at 15% annually. However, despite these advancements, many recruitment agencies, particularly small-to-medium enterprises (SMEs) in developing economies, still rely heavily on manual processes.

South Africa presents a unique recruitment landscape characterised by high unemployment rates (32.9% as of Q1 2026, Statistics South Africa) and a highly competitive talent market. Recruitment agencies face the challenge of processing thousands of applications per vacancy while maintaining quality and speed. This tension between volume and quality creates operational bottlenecks that directly impact revenue, as most agencies operate on a contingency basis (payment only upon successful placement).

### **1.1.2 The Rise of AI in Human Resources**

Artificial Intelligence has emerged as a transformative force in Human Resources. According to Deloitte's 2025 Global Human Capital Trends report, 67% of organisations have implemented or are planning to implement AI-powered recruitment tools. Key applications include:

* **Resume Screening:** AI algorithms that automatically parse and evaluate CVs against job requirements  
* **Candidate Matching:** Systems that match candidates to roles based on skills, experience, and cultural fit  
* **Chatbots:** Automated candidate engagement and interview scheduling  
* **Predictive Analytics:** Forecasting candidate success and retention

However, the adoption of AI in recruitment has been uneven, with larger enterprises leading the way while smaller agencies lag due to cost, complexity, and lack of technical expertise.

### **1.1.3 Tumaini Consulting: A Case Study in Recruitment Challenges**

Tumaini Consulting, founded in 2015 by Nandi KaNjomane KaBhebhe, represents the typical South African recruitment SME. The company's name, "Tumaini," means "Hope" in Swahili – a philosophy that guides every endeavour. Despite its strong values and committed team of 10-15 employees, the company faces significant operational challenges:

* The processing of over 25,000 CVs annually;  
* Significant time investment, with recruiters spending between 5 and 15 hours per role on manual screening;  
* Inconsistent shortlisting, exhibiting a 45% variance between individual recruiters;  
* A 70% adherence rate to the published three-day candidate response standard; and  
* An accumulation of over 15,000 historical CVs stored in unusable formats across email archives and shared drives.

Nandi KaNjomane KaBhebhe articulated the challenge:

*"We have never tried to be the biggest agency. We try to be the best. That means delivering quality candidates to clients and presenting our candidates with the best opportunities. But being the best also means delivering shortlists quickly and accurately – and right now, manual CV screening is our biggest bottleneck."*

This research was motivated by Tumaini Consulting's need for an AI-powered solution that would honour their name by delivering hope through faster, fairer, and more transparent recruitment.

### **1.1.4 Technological Foundations**

Recent advances in artificial intelligence offer promising solutions to recruitment challenges:

**Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG):** RAG combines information retrieval with large language models (LLMs) to generate contextually relevant responses. In recruitment, RAG can retrieve relevant CV sections and generate explainable match scores.

**Vector Databases:** Unlike traditional keyword search, vector databases store embeddings (numerical representations) of text, enabling semantic similarity search. This means "Python developer" can match candidates who wrote "software engineer with Python expertise" – something keyword search cannot do.

**Domain-Driven Design (DDD):** DDD provides a methodology for modelling complex domains. The recruitment domain has inherent complexity: Users, CVs, Jobs, Applications, Shortlists, and Matching. DDD helps manage this complexity by defining bounded contexts and aggregates.

**Microservices Architecture:** Microservices decompose applications into independently deployable services. This approach enables teams to develop, deploy, and scale services independently.

## 

## 

## **1.2 Problem Statement**

### **1.2.1 Core Problem**

Tumaini Consulting faces a critical operational bottleneck: **the manual screening of high-volume CVs is unsustainable, inconsistent, and prevents scalable growth.**

This core problem manifests in several specific challenges:

**Challenge 1: Excessive Time Investment**

Recruiters at Tumaini Consulting spend between 5 and 15 hours per role manually reviewing CVs. For a role with 1,500 applicants, a single recruiter spends approximately 18-25 hours on initial screening alone – before any interviews or client feedback. This time investment is unsustainable for a lean team of 10-15 employees.

**Challenge 2: Inconsistent Shortlisting**

A blind test conducted in October 2025 revealed that four recruiters independently reviewing the same 400 CVs produced shortlists with 45% variance. Only 7 candidates appeared on all four shortlists. This inconsistency undermines client trust and creates compliance risks for enterprise clients.

**Challenge 3: Failed Automation Attempts**

Tumaini Consulting has attempted several low-cost automation approaches:

* Boolean keyword search: Excluded qualified candidates using different terminology  
* Manual candidate tagging: Abandoned after 3 months due to time constraints  
* Basic CV parsing tools: Failed on 35% of CVs due to formatting variations  
* Spreadsheet-based scoring: Slower than manual review, rejected by recruiters

**Challenge 4: Untapped Historical Data**

The company has accumulated over 15,000 historical CVs stored across email archives, shared drives, and individual recruiter folders. Without semantic search capabilities, these records are effectively unusable. Recruiters start from scratch for each new role, even when suitable candidates exist in historical records.

**Challenge 5: 3-Day Response Standard Not Met**

Tumaini Consulting has a published standard: *"Correspondence will only be conducted with short listed candidates. Should you not hear from us within 3 days, please consider your application unsuccessful."* However, current adherence is only 70%, leading to candidate frustration and lost talent.

**Challenge 6: Lack of Explainability**

Enterprise clients increasingly require auditable shortlisting processes. They demand to know why specific candidates were shortlisted and what evidence supports that decision. Manual processes cannot provide this transparency.

### **1.2.2 Technical Gap**

Beyond the operational challenges, there exists a technical gap in the literature and practice:

1. **Limited research on RAG for recruitment:** While RAG has been extensively studied for question-answering and document retrieval, its application to candidate matching with explainable scoring is under-researched.  
2. **Vector database adoption in SMEs:** Most research on vector databases focuses on large-scale enterprise applications. There is limited guidance on deploying vector databases for small-to-medium recruitment agencies.  
3. **DDD for recruitment domains:** While DDD is well-established, there are few documented case studies of its application to recruitment systems with multiple bounded contexts.  
4. **POPIA-compliant AI in South Africa:** The Protection of Personal Information Act (POPIA) imposes specific requirements on AI systems processing South African citizen data. There is limited literature on designing POPIA-compliant AI recruitment systems.

### **1.2.3 Consequence of Inaction**

Without intervention, Tumaini Consulting faces:

* Continued recruiter burnout and turnover (current voluntary turnover: 25%)  
* Loss of enterprise clients who demand transparent, auditable processes  
* Inability to scale beyond current volume (25,000 CVs annually)  
* Missed candidates due to fatigue and inconsistency  
* Competitive disadvantage against larger agencies with AI capabilities

## **1.3 Research Questions**

This research addresses the following primary research question:

**RQ1:** How can an AI-powered recruitment platform using Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) and vector databases reduce manual CV screening time for a small-to-medium recruitment agency?

The following secondary research questions guide the investigation:

**RQ2:** What is the optimal microservices architecture and Domain-Driven Design (DDD) strategy for modelling the recruitment domain (Users, CVs, Jobs, Applications, Shortlists, Matching)?

**RQ3:** How can semantic search using vector embeddings improve candidate matching accuracy compared to keyword-based search in a recruitment context?

**RQ4:** How can RAG be implemented to provide explainable, auditable match scores for enterprise clients requiring transparency in shortlisting decisions?

**RQ5:** How can the system be architected to comply with South Africa's Protection of Personal Information Act (POPIA) while using cloud-based AI services?

**RQ6:** What is the effectiveness of Agile Scrum methodology with 2-week sprints for delivering a complex AI system within a 16-week academic timeline?

## **1.4 Aim and Objectives**

### **1.4.1 Aim**

The aim of this research is to design, develop, and evaluate an AI-powered recruitment platform for Tumaini Consulting that automates CV screening and candidate matching using Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG), vector databases, and semantic search, while following Domain-Driven Design (DDD) principles and microservices architecture.

### **1.4.2 Objectives**

The following objectives guide the research:

**Objective 1 (O1):** To analyse the current recruitment challenges at Tumaini Consulting and derive functional and non-functional requirements for an AI-powered solution.

**Objective 2 (O2):** To design a microservices architecture with five bounded contexts (Identity, CV Management, Vector Search, Matching, Job & Shortlist) using Domain-Driven Design principles.

**Objective 3 (O3):** To implement a CV parsing and information extraction pipeline supporting multiple formats (PDF, DOCX, TXT, RTF) with OCR fallback for scanned documents.

**Objective 4 (O4):** To implement a vector database (Qdrant) for storing candidate embeddings and enabling semantic similarity search across 15,000+ historical CVs.

**Objective 5 (O5):** To implement a Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) pipeline using LLMs (GPT-4o-mini with Llama 3 fallback) for candidate matching with explainable scores.

**Objective 6 (O6):** To develop a React-based frontend with separate portals for candidates (job search, application, status tracking) and recruiters (job management, candidate search, shortlist management).

**Objective 7 (O7):** To evaluate the system against success metrics including time-to-shortlist, screening time reduction, 3-day response adherence, and shortlist consistency.

**Objective 8 (O8):** To deploy the system on AWS af-south-1 (Cape Town) with full POPIA compliance and deliver comprehensive documentation for Tumaini Consulting.

## **1.5 Scope and Delimitations**

### **1.5.1 Scope**

The project scope includes:

**In Scope:**

* Development of five microservices (Identity, CV, Vector, Matching, Job)  
* Candidate portal for registration, CV upload, job search, and application tracking  
* Recruiter portal for job management, semantic candidate search, and shortlist management  
* AI-powered CV parsing and NER extraction for South African candidate data  
* Semantic search using vector embeddings  
* RAG-based candidate matching with explainable scores  
* Automated 3-day response notifications  
* PDF and Excel shortlist export  
* Kong API gateway with JWT authentication and rate limiting  
* Deployment on AWS af-south-1 (Cape Town)  
* Comprehensive documentation (user manuals, admin guide, deployment guide)

**Out of Scope:**

* Integration with existing client ATS systems (API ready for future)  
* Mobile native applications (web responsive only)  
* Video interviewing or assessment tools  
* Payroll or onboarding functionality  
* Advanced analytics dashboard beyond basic KPIs  
* LinkedIn or social media integration  
* Automated interview scheduling

### **1.5.2 Delimitations**

The following delimitations define the boundaries of this research:

1. **Client Limitation:** The system is designed specifically for Tumaini Consulting and may require modification for other recruitment agencies.  
2. **Geographic Limitation:** While the system is deployed on AWS af-south-1, it could theoretically serve other African regions, but specific adaptations may be required.  
3. **Language Limitation:** The NER system is optimised for English CVs with South African terminology. Other languages are not supported.  
4. **Temporal Limitation:** The project was completed within a 16-week academic timeline (August – November 2026), which constrained certain features (e.g., advanced analytics, client portals).  
5. **Data Limitation:** The system was trained and tested on CVs provided by Tumaini Consulting. Performance on other CV formats may vary.  
6. **LLM Limitation:** The RAG pipeline uses GPT-4o-mini as the primary LLM and Llama 3 as fallback. Other LLMs were not evaluated.

## **1.6 Contribution to Knowledge**

This research makes the following contributions to knowledge:

### **1.6.1 Academic Contributions**

1. **RAG for Recruitment:** Extends the application of Retrieval-Augmented Generation from general question-answering to the specific domain of candidate matching with explainable scores, contributing a novel prompt engineering approach and evaluation methodology.  
2. **DDD for Recruitment Domain:** Provides a documented case study of applying Domain-Driven Design to a recruitment system with five bounded contexts (Identity, CV Management, Vector Search, Matching, Job & Shortlist), including event storming outputs and context mapping.  
3. **Vector Database Benchmark for Recruitment:** Contributes performance benchmarks for Qdrant with 15,000-20,000 vectors in a recruitment context, including latency and recall metrics.  
4. **South African NER Enhancement:** Extends existing NER models with South African-specific entities (SA ID numbers, phone number formats, NQF levels, professional certifications like GCC Mines & Works).

### **1.6.2 Practical Contributions**

1. **Working System:** A fully functional AI-powered recruitment platform deployed for Tumaini Consulting, directly addressing their operational challenges.  
2. **Open-Source Components:** Key components (CV parsers, embedding service, RAG pipeline) will be open-sourced for the benefit of other South African recruitment SMEs.  
3. **POPIA Compliance Framework:** A documented framework for designing POPIA-compliant AI systems using AWS af-south-1 and local LLM fallbacks.  
4. **Agile Documentation:** Complete Agile documentation including product backlog, burndown charts, sprint reports, and retrospectives that can serve as a template for future academic-industry projects.

### **1.6.3 Social Contributions**

1. **Employment Access:** By improving Tumaini Consulting's efficiency, the platform indirectly helps more South Africans find employment faster.  
2. **Fairer Recruitment:** The explainable AI scores reduce unconscious bias compared to manual screening, promoting fairer candidate evaluation.  
3. **SME Empowerment:** Provides a reference architecture for other South African SMEs considering AI adoption, democratising access to advanced recruitment technology.

## **1.7 Dissertation Structure**

This dissertation is organised into nine chapters:

**Chapter 1: Introduction** – Presents the background, problem statement, research questions, aims, objectives, scope, and contributions.

**Chapter 2: Literature Review** – Reviews existing literature on AI in HR, RAG, vector databases, DDD, microservices, Agile, and POPIA.

**Chapter 3: Methodology** – Describes the Agile Scrum methodology, DDD approach, microservices architecture, technology stack, sprint structure, and quality assurance strategy.

**Chapter 4: Requirements Analysis** – Presents the client profile, current challenges, functional and non-functional requirements, user stories, event storming, and bounded contexts.

**Chapter 5: System Design** – Details the high-level architecture, microservices design, DDD implementation, database design, vector database design, RAG pipeline design, API design, frontend architecture, and security architecture.

**Chapter 6: Implementation** – Documents the development across eight sprints, including key technical decisions, code examples, and screenshots.

**Chapter 7: Testing and Evaluation** – Presents the testing strategy, unit tests, integration tests, E2E tests, performance tests, security tests, UAT, and evaluation against objectives.

**Chapter 8: Discussion** – Interprets the findings, addresses research questions, compares with existing systems, presents challenges and lessons learned, and includes the Agile retrospective.

**Chapter 9: Conclusion and Future Work** – Summarises contributions, discusses limitations, presents recommendations for future work, and concludes the dissertation.

**Appendices** – Include the RFP document, TRD, product backlog, burndown charts, test reports, user manuals, deployment guide, and lessons learned document.

---

**Chapter 2: Literature Review**

## **2.1 Introduction to Recruitment Technology**

### **2.1.1 Evolution of Recruitment Technology**

The recruitment technology landscape has evolved significantly over the past three decades. Figure 2.1 illustrates this evolution.

![][image1]  
**1990s – The Paper Era:** Recruitment was primarily conducted through newspaper advertisements and physical job postings. Candidates submitted paper CVs, and recruiters manually screened each document. A single vacancy could generate hundreds of paper CVs, requiring significant physical storage and manual effort.

**2000s – Digital Transition:** The emergence of online job boards (Monster, CareerBuilder, Pnet in South Africa) shifted applications to email and online forms. Applicant Tracking Systems (ATS) emerged, offering basic keyword search functionality. However, these systems were expensive and primarily adopted by large enterprises.

**2010s – Cloud and Mobile:** Cloud-based ATS (Lever, Greenhouse, Workday) democratised access to recruitment technology. Social recruiting (LinkedIn) became mainstream. Mobile applications allowed candidates to apply from smartphones. Analytics dashboards provided basic metrics on source effectiveness and time-to-hire.

**2020s – AI Era:** The current era is defined by artificial intelligence. AI-powered screening, automated candidate matching, chatbots, and predictive analytics are transforming recruitment. Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) and vector databases represent the cutting edge, enabling semantic search and explainable AI.

### **2.1.2 Current State of Recruitment Technology in South Africa**

The South African recruitment technology market is characterised by:

**Adoption Gap:** Large enterprises (banks, telecoms, mining companies) have adopted sophisticated ATS and AI tools. However, small-to-medium recruitment agencies like Tumaini Consulting often rely on manual processes or basic ATS due to cost and complexity.

**Local Solutions:** Several South African recruitment technology vendors operate in the market, including:

* **Pnet:** The largest job board in South Africa, with ATS functionality  
* **CareerJunction:** Another major job board with recruitment tools  
* **SmartMatch:** AI-powered candidate matching for South African employers

**Regulatory Environment:** The Protection of Personal Information Act (POPIA), effective since 2021, imposes significant requirements on recruitment technology vendors and users. Data must be stored within South African borders or in countries with equivalent protection. Candidates have the right to access, correct, and delete their data.

### **2.1.3 Limitations of Existing Systems**

Despite advancements, existing recruitment systems have significant limitations:

**Keyword Dependence:** Traditional ATS rely on keyword matching. A candidate who writes "customer engagement" instead of "customer relationship management" may be excluded, despite identical skills.

**Black Box AI:** Many AI recruitment tools provide scores without explanation. Recruiters cannot understand why a candidate received a particular score, undermining trust and creating compliance risks.

**Limited South African Context:** Most AI recruitment tools are developed in North America or Europe and lack awareness of South African-specific entities (SA ID numbers, NQF levels, professional certifications like GCC Mines & Works).

**Cost Prohibitive:** Enterprise-grade AI recruitment tools cost thousands of dollars per month, placing them out of reach for SMEs.

**Data Sovereignty:** Many AI vendors store data on US servers, violating POPIA requirements for data residency.

## **2.2 Artificial Intelligence in Human Resources**

### **2.2.1 Overview of AI in HR**

Artificial Intelligence in Human Resources (AI-HR) encompasses applications of machine learning, natural language processing, and computer vision to HR functions. According to a 2025 survey by Mercer, the most common AI-HR applications are:

| Application | Adoption Rate | Primary Benefit |
| ----- | :---: | ----- |
| Resume Screening | 58% | Time reduction |
| Candidate Matching | 45% | Quality improvement |
| Chatbots (candidate engagement) | 42% | Response speed |
| Interview scheduling | 38% | Efficiency |
| Predictive attrition | 25% | Retention |
| Video interview analysis | 18% | Bias reduction |

### **2.2.2 Resume Screening AI**

Resume screening is the most widely adopted AI-HR application. Traditional resume screening uses keyword matching, while modern approaches use natural language processing (NLP) and machine learning.

**Keyword-Based Screening:** The system maintains a list of keywords relevant to the job. Candidates are scored based on keyword frequency. Limitations include:

* Synonym blindness (e.g., "Java" vs "J2EE")  
* Context unawareness ("Java" the island vs "Java" the programming language)  
* Susceptibility to keyword stuffing (candidates adding irrelevant keywords)

**NLP-Based Screening:** NLP techniques extract structured information from unstructured CV text:

* Named Entity Recognition (NER) identifies names, dates, skills, and organisations  
* Text classification categorises CVs into job categories  
* Similarity scoring matches CVs to job descriptions

**LLM-Based Screening:** Large Language Models (GPT-4, Llama 3\) represent the cutting edge. Unlike traditional NLP, LLMs understand context and can generate natural-language explanations for their decisions.

### **2.2.3 Explainable AI (XAI) in Recruitment**

Explainable AI is critical in recruitment for several reasons:

**Legal Compliance:** In many jurisdictions, including South Africa, candidates have the right to understand why they were rejected. The Employment Equity Act prohibits discrimination, and unexplained AI decisions could mask bias.

**Client Trust:** Enterprise clients demand transparency. They need to justify shortlisting decisions to their internal stakeholders and compliance teams.

**System Improvement:** Recruiter feedback on AI decisions (e.g., "this candidate was underrated") can be used to improve models.

The RAG architecture used in this project provides explainability by generating natural-language rationales for each match score, citing specific evidence from the candidate's CV.

### **2.2.4 Bias and Fairness in AI Recruitment**

AI recruitment systems can perpetuate or amplify existing biases if not carefully designed. Key considerations include:

**Data Bias:** If training data contains historical biases (e.g., more male engineers), the AI may learn to prefer male candidates.

**Algorithmic Bias:** The algorithm itself may treat certain attributes as proxies for protected characteristics (e.g., postal code as proxy for race).

**Evaluation Bias:** Human evaluation of AI systems may be biased, accepting errors that favour certain groups more than others.

Mitigation strategies employed in this project:

* **Explainable outputs:** Recruiters can see why a candidate received a score  
* **Human-in-the-loop:** Recruiters can override AI shortlists  
* **Bias audit:** Regular reviews of score distributions by demographic group (where data available)  
* **Transparency:** All AI decisions are auditable

## **2.3 Natural Language Processing for Resume Parsing**

### **2.3.1 Resume Parsing Challenges**

Resume parsing is the process of extracting structured information from unstructured CV text. This is a challenging NLP task due to:

**Format Variety:** CVs can be PDF, DOCX, TXT, RTF, or scanned images. Each format requires different parsing approaches.

**Layout Variation:** CV layouts vary widely – two-column, multi-column, tables, graphics, embedded images.

**Language Variation:** Candidates use different terminology for the same skills. South African CVs often include local terms ("Matric", "NQF level 7", "GCC Mines & Works").

**Noise:** CVs contain inconsistent formatting, spelling errors, abbreviations, and ambiguous dates.

### **2.3.2 Named Entity Recognition (NER) for CVs**

NER identifies and classifies named entities in text. For CV parsing, relevant entities include:

| Entity Type | Examples |
| :---: | :---: |
| PERSON | 'Thabo Nkosi', 'Nkosi, Thabo' |
| EMAIL | 'thabo@example.com' |
| PHONE | '+27 82 123 4567', '082 123 4567' |
| LOCATION | 'Cape Town', 'Johannesburg' |
| DATE | 'January 2020', '2020-2024' |
| ORGANISATION | 'ABC Corp', 'University of Cape Town' |
| SKILL | 'Python', 'AWS', 'Spring Boot' |
| DEGREE | 'BSc Computer Science', 'MBA' |
| CERTIFICATION | 'AWS Certified Solutions Architect', 'GCC Mines & Works'  |

**spaCy NER Model:** This project used spaCy's `en_core_web_sm` model, augmented with custom patterns for South African entities. The model achieved:

* Name extraction accuracy: 95%  
* Email extraction accuracy: 99%  
* Phone extraction accuracy: 90% (SA formats)  
* Skill extraction recall: 85%

### 

### 

### **2.3.3 Optical Character Recognition (OCR) for Scanned CVs**

Some candidates submit scanned PDFs (image-based, no selectable text). OCR is required to extract text from these documents.

**Tesseract OCR:** Tesseract is an open-source OCR engine with support for multiple languages. For this project, we installed English, Afrikaans, and isiZulu language packs to support South African language diversity.

**OCR Pipeline:**

1. Convert PDF to images (one per page)  
2. Preprocess images (deskew, denoise, threshold)  
3. Apply Tesseract OCR with language detection  
4. Concatenate extracted text across pages

**Performance:** OCR accuracy on clean scanned documents: \>80% word accuracy. Degraded documents (faded, handwritten notes) had lower accuracy, and such CVs were flagged for manual review.

## **2.4 Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG)**

### **2.4.1 Overview of RAG**

Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) is an architecture that combines information retrieval with large language models (LLMs). First introduced by Lewis et al. (2020) in the paper "Retrieval-Augmented Generation for Knowledge-Intensive NLP Tasks," RAG addresses a key limitation of LLMs: their inability to access or recall specific information not present in their training data.

**Traditional LLM Generation:** The LLM generates responses based solely on its parametric knowledge (weights). Limitations include:

* Hallucination (inventing facts)  
* Outdated knowledge (training data cut-off)  
* Inability to cite sources

**RAG Generation:** The LLM is augmented with retrieved documents:

1. **Retrieve:** Relevant documents are retrieved from a knowledge base using similarity search  
2. **Augment:** The retrieved documents are added to the prompt as context  
3. **Generate:** The LLM generates a response conditioned on both the prompt and retrieved context

![][image2]

### **2.4.2 RAG for Candidate Matching**

In the recruitment context, RAG is applied as follows:

**Retrieve:** For a given job description, retrieve the top-K candidate CVs from the vector database. The vector search finds semantically similar candidates, not just keyword matches.

**Augment:** Build a prompt that includes:

* System message defining the role and output format  
* Job description (title, requirements, responsibilities)  
* Candidate CV snippets (skills, experience, education)

**Generate:** The LLM evaluates the candidate against the job description and outputs a JSON object containing:

* `score`: Integer from 0-100  
* `rationale`: Natural-language explanation  
* `matched_skills`: List of skills the candidate has that match requirements  
* `missing_skills`: List of required skills the candidate lacks

### **2.4.3 LLM Selection for RAG**

This project evaluated several LLMs for the RAG pipeline:

| Model | Provider | Cost | Strengths | Weaknesses |
| :---: | :---: | :---: | :---: | :---: |
| GPT-4o-mini | OpenAI | Low | Fast, accurate, JSON mode | Data leaves SA |
| Llama 3 (8B) | Meta (Ollama) | Free | Local, POPIA compliant | Slower, lower accuracy |
| GPT-3.5 Turbo | OpenAI | Very low | Fast | Lower accuracy, no JSON mode |
| Claude 3 Haiku | Anthropic | Low | Good reasoning | Data leaves SA |

**Selected Architecture:**

* **Primary:** GPT-4o-mini (cost-effective, good quality, JSON mode for reliable parsing)  
* **Fallback:** Llama 3 via Ollama (local, free, POPIA-compliant when primary unavailable)

**Temperature Setting:** 0.2 (low variability) to ensure deterministic, reproducible outputs.

### **2.4.4 Prompt Engineering for Candidate Matching**

Prompt engineering is critical for RAG performance. The prompt template evolved through multiple iterations:

**Initial Prompt (Version 1):**

| Rate this candidate for the job. Output a score from 0-100. |
| :---- |

**Iterated Prompt (Version 5 – Final):**

| You are an expert recruitment AI for Tumaini Consulting in South Africa.JOB DESCRIPTION:Title: {job\_title}Requirements: {requirements}Responsibilities: {responsibilities}Required Skills: {required\_skills}CANDIDATE CV:{cv\_snippets} |
| :---- |

INSTRUCTIONS:  
1\. Evaluate the candidate against the job requirements  
2\. Output ONLY valid JSON with the following structure:

| {  "score": integer between 0-100,  "rationale": "specific evidence from CV",  "matched\_skills": \["skill1", "skill2"\],  "missing\_skills": \["skill3", "skill4"\]} |
| :---- |

SCORING RUBRIC:  
\- 90-100: Perfect match, all requirements met  
\- 70-89: Strong match, most requirements met  
\- 50-69: Partial match, some requirements met  
\- 0-49: Weak match, few requirements met

Consider South African context (NQF levels, SAQA qualifications, GCC certifications).  
Be objective and cite specific CV evidence.

### **2.4.5 RAG Evaluation**

The RAG pipeline was evaluated on 50 job descriptions and 500 candidates:

| Metric | Target | Achieved |
| ----- | :---: | :---: |
| JSON parsing success rate | \>99%  | 99.4% |
| Score correlation with human r | \>0.7  | r=0.76 |
| Response time (20 candidates) | \<30s  | 24s |
| Fallback activation rate | \<5%  | 2% |

## **2.5 Vector Databases and Semantic Search**

### **2.5.1 From Keyword Search to Semantic Search**

Traditional keyword search matches exact words or stems. This approach fails when candidates use different terminology for the same concept.

**Keyword Search Example:**

* Query: "Java developer with Spring Boot"  
* Candidate writes: "Software engineer with Java and Spring Framework"  
* Keyword match: Partial (Java matches, "Spring Framework" ≠ "Spring Boot")  
* Result: Candidate may be missed

**Semantic Search:**

* Query converted to vector embedding  
* Candidate CVs converted to vector embeddings  
* Cosine similarity calculated  
* Candidate with "Spring Framework" is found because the embeddings are close

### 

### 

### **2.5.2 Vector Embeddings**

Vector embeddings are numerical representations of text. Similar texts have similar vectors (closer in vector space).

**Embedding Models:** This project used `sentence-transformers/all-MiniLM-L6-v2`, which produces 384-dimensional embeddings. This model was chosen for:

* **Size:** 22MB (small enough for local deployment)  
* **Speed:** 1,000 documents/second on CPU  
* **Accuracy:** 86% on Semantic Textual Similarity (STS) benchmark  
* **Open-source:** Free for commercial use

**Embedding Generation:**

python  
from sentence\_transformers import SentenceTransformer  
model \= SentenceTransformer('all-MiniLM-L6-v2')  
embedding \= model.encode("Python developer with AWS experience")  
\# embedding shape: (384,)

### **2.5.3 Vector Database Comparison**

Several vector databases were evaluated for this project:

| Database | Open Source | HNSW Support | Self-Hostable | POPIA Compliance | Selected |
| :---: | :---: | :---: | :---: | :---: | :---: |
| Qdrant | Yes | Yes | Yes | Yes | ✅ Selected |
| Pinecone | No (free tier) | Yes | No | No | ❌ Rejected |
| Weaviate | Yes | Yes | Yes | Yes | Considered |
| Milvus | Yes | Yes | Yes | Yes | Considered |
| Chroma | Yes | Yes | Yes | Yes | Considered |

**Why Qdrant was selected:**

* Fully open-source, allowing self-hosting on AWS af-south-1  
* Excellent performance with HNSW indexing (\<200ms at 20,000 vectors)  
* Rich filtering capabilities (filter by sector, experience years)  
* Python client with good documentation  
* Active development community

### **2.5.4 HNSW Indexing**

Hierarchical Navigable Small World (HNSW) is the state-of-the-art algorithm for approximate nearest neighbour search. It builds a multi-layer graph structure that enables fast search with high recall.

**Configuration used in this project:**

| Parameter | Value | Effect |
| :---: | :---: | :---: |
| ef\_construct | 100 | Build quality (higher \= better recall, slower build) |
| M | 16 | Connections per node (higher \= better recall) |
| ef | 50 | Search quality (higher \= better recall, slower search) |

**Performance achieved:**

* Build time: 5 seconds for 20,000 vectors  
* Search latency: 185ms P95 at 20,000 vectors  
* Recall rate: 0.96 (at ef=50)

## **2.6 Domain-Driven Design (DDD)**

### **2.6.1 Overview of DDD**

Domain-Driven Design, introduced by Eric Evans in his 2003 book, provides a methodology for modelling complex domains. DDD is particularly valuable when the domain is complex and the business rules are non-trivial – characteristics true of recruitment.

**Core DDD Concepts:**

![][image3]

### **2.6.2 Bounded Contexts for Recruitment**

A bounded context is a boundary within which a particular model is defined and applicable. For the recruitment domain, five bounded contexts were identified:

**Context 1: Identity & Access**

* **Responsibility:** User authentication, authorisation, role management  
* **Aggregate:** User  
* **Value Objects:** Email, Password, Role  
* **Domain Events:** UserRegistered, UserLoggedIn, UserRoleChanged

**Context 2: CV Management**

* **Responsibility:** CV upload, storage, parsing, NER extraction  
* **Aggregate:** CurriculumVitae  
* **Entities:** WorkExperience, Education, Certification  
* **Domain Events:** CVUploaded, CVProcessed, CVFailed

**Context 3: Vector Search**

* **Responsibility:** Embedding generation, vector storage, semantic search  
* **Aggregate:** VectorEmbedding  
* **Value Objects:** Embedding, Metadata  
* **Domain Events:** EmbeddingGenerated, EmbeddingDeleted

**Context 4: Matching**

* **Responsibility:** RAG pipeline, candidate scoring, explainable rationales  
* **Aggregate:** MatchingJob  
* **Entity:** MatchingResult  
* **Domain Events:** MatchStarted, MatchCompleted, MatchFailed

**Context 5: Job & Shortlist**

* **Responsibility:** Job posting, applications, shortlist management  
* **Aggregates:** JobPosting, Shortlist  
* **Entity:** Application  
* **Domain Events:** JobPosted, ApplicationSubmitted, ShortlistCreated

### **2.6.3 Aggregates and Entities**

**Aggregate:** A cluster of domain objects treated as a single unit. Each aggregate has a root entity (the aggregate root) that is the only entry point for external access.

**Aggregate Root Examples:**

* `User` (contains `Session` and `RefreshToken` entities)  
* `CurriculumVitae` (contains `WorkExperience`, `Education`, `Certification` entities)  
* `JobPosting` (contains `Application` entities)

**Value Objects:** Immutable objects defined by their attributes rather than identity.

* `Email` (validation, case-insensitive comparison)  
* `Password` (bcrypt hashing, never stored plaintext)  
* `Score` (0-100 with confidence interval)

### **2.6.4 Domain Events**

Domain events capture significant occurrences within the domain. They enable loose coupling between bounded contexts.

**Example Domain Events:**

* `UserRegistered`: Published by Identity context, consumed by CV context (create empty CV record)  
* `CVProcessed`: Published by CV context, consumed by Vector context (generate embedding)  
* `MatchCompleted`: Published by Matching context, consumed by Job context (create shortlist)

### **2.6.5 Repository Pattern**

The repository pattern abstracts data persistence, allowing the domain layer to remain independent of database technology.

| class UserRepository(ABC):    @abstractmethod    async def add(self, user: User) \-\> None:        pass        @abstractmethod    async def get(self, user\_id: UUID) \-\> Optional\[User\]:        pass        @abstractmethod    async def get\_by\_email(self, email: Email) \-\> Optional\[User\]:        pass |
| :---- |

## **2.7 Microservices Architecture**

### **2.7.1 Microservices vs Monolithic Architecture**

Monolithic applications bundle all functionality into a single deployable unit. Microservices decompose the application into independently deployable services.

| Aspect | Monolithic | Microservices |
| ----- | ----- | ----- |
| Deployment | One unit | Multiple units |
| Scalability | Scale entire app | Scale individual services |
| Technology | Uniform | Polyglot |
| Development | Large team coordination | Small team autonomy |
| Complexity | Low initial, high later | High initial, lower later |

**Why Microservices for Recruitment:** The recruitment domain has clear boundaries (Identity, CVs, Search, Matching, Jobs). Each bounded context can be implemented as a separate microservice, enabling independent development, scaling, and deployment.

### **2.7.2 Microservices Communication**

Services communicate through two primary patterns:

**Synchronous (Request-Response):** Used when immediate response is required.

* Example: Recruiter searches candidates  
* API Gateway routes to Vector service → returns results  
* Protocol: HTTP/REST

**Asynchronous (Event-Driven):** Used when eventual consistency is acceptable.

* Example: CV uploaded → embedding generation needed  
* CV service publishes `CVUploaded` event  
* Vector service consumes event and generates embeddings  
* Protocol: RabbitMQ (AMQP)

### **2.7.3 API Gateway Pattern**

The API Gateway is a single entry point for all client requests. It handles:

* **Request routing:** Directs requests to the appropriate microservice  
* **Authentication:** Validates JWT before forwarding  
* **Rate limiting:** Prevents abuse  
* **Logging and metrics:** Central observability

**Kong Gateway Configuration:**

| services:\- name: identity-service  url: http://identity-service:8001  routes:  \- name: auth-routes    paths:    \- /api/auth    strip\_path: true\- name: cv-service  url: http://cv-service:8002  routes:  \- name: cv-routes    paths:    \- /api/cvs    strip\_path: true |
| :---- |

### 

### **2.7.4 Service Discovery**

In Docker Compose (development), services discover each other by service name (e.g., `http://identity-service:8001`).

In production (Kubernetes), Kubernetes DNS provides service discovery.

### **2.7.5 Inter-Service Communication Challenges**

**Challenge 1: Distributed Transactions:** Maintaining consistency across services. Solution: Eventual consistency with compensating events.

**Challenge 2: Network Latency:** Network calls are slower than in-memory calls. Solution: Caching (Redis) to reduce repeated calls.

**Challenge 3: Service Failure:** One service failing should not cascade. Solution: Circuit breakers, retries, fallbacks.

## **2.8 Agile Software Development**

### **2.8.1 Agile Principles**

The Agile Manifesto (2001) values:

* **Individuals and interactions** over processes and tools  
* **Working software** over comprehensive documentation  
* **Customer collaboration** over contract negotiation  
* **Responding to change** over following a plan

### **2.8.2 Scrum Framework**

Scrum is the most widely used Agile framework. Key elements:

**Roles:**

* **Product Owner:** Represents stakeholders, prioritises backlog  
* **Scrum Master:** Facilitates ceremonies, removes impediments  
* **Development Team:** Self-organising, cross-functional (3-9 members)

**Artifacts:**

* **Product Backlog:** Ordered list of desired functionality  
* **Sprint Backlog:** Subset of backlog for current sprint  
* **Increment:** Working software at sprint end

**Ceremonies:**

* **Sprint Planning:** Define sprint goal and select backlog items (2 hours per 2-week sprint)  
* **Daily Stand-up:** 15-minute daily coordination  
* **Sprint Review:** Demo increment to stakeholders (1 hour)  
* **Sprint Retrospective:** Reflect on process, identify improvements (1 hour)

### **2.8.3 Agile for Academic-Industry Projects**

Agile is particularly suitable for academic-industry projects because:

**Adaptability:** Requirements evolve as understanding deepens. Tumaini Consulting's requirements were refined throughout the project.

**Regular Feedback:** Sprint reviews provided opportunities for client feedback, preventing major surprises.

**Visibility:** Burndown charts and velocity tracking provided transparent progress reporting.

**Risk Management:** Early sprints delivered infrastructure, de-risking complex AI components.

### **2.8.4 JIRA as Agile Tool**

JIRA Software was used to manage the project:

**Configuration:**

* Issue types: Epic, Story, Task, Bug, Sub-task  
* Workflow: Backlog → To Do → In Progress → In Review → Done  
* Custom fields: Story Points (Fibonacci), Sprint, Priority

**Reports Generated:**

* Burndown charts (actual vs planned progress)  
* Velocity chart (story points completed per sprint)  
* Sprint reports (completed vs incomplete stories)

**Sprint Structure (8 sprints x 2 weeks):**

| Sprint | Focus | Points |
| :---: | ----- | :---: |
| 1 | Shared Kernel & Infrastructure | 15 |
| 2 | Identity & Access Management | 20 |
| 3 | CV Management Service | 25 |
| 4 | Vector & Search Service | 25 |
| 5 | Matching & RAG Service | 30 |
| 6 | Job & Shortlist Service | 25 |
| 7 | React Frontend & API Gateway | 45 |
| 8 | Testing, Deployment & Handover | 15 |

## **2.9 POPIA and Data Privacy in South Africa**

### **2.9.1 Overview of POPIA**

The Protection of Personal Information Act (POPIA) is South Africa's data protection law, effective from 1 July 2021\. It is broadly aligned with the EU's General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR).

**Key Principles:**

| Principle | Description |
| :---: | :---: |
| Accountability | Responsible party must ensure compliance |
| Processing limitation | Collect only necessary data |
| Purpose specification | Collect for specified, lawful purposes |
| Further processing limitation | Process only for original purpose |
| Information quality | Keep data accurate and up-to-date |
| Openness | Maintain documentation of processing |
| Security safeguards | Protect data from loss, damage, unauthorised access |
| Data subject participation | Data subjects can access, correct, delete their data |

### **2.9.2 POPIA Requirements for AI Recruitment Systems**

**Data Residency:** Personal information must not be transferred outside South Africa unless the receiving country has adequate protection laws or binding corporate rules apply.

**Implication for this project:** All candidate data (CVs, personal information) must be stored on servers physically located in South Africa. AWS af-south-1 (Cape Town) satisfies this requirement.

**Data Subject Rights:** Candidates have the right to:

* Access their personal information  
* Request correction of inaccurate information  
* Request deletion of their information  
* Object to processing  
* Withdraw consent

**Implication for this project:** The system must provide mechanisms for candidates to exercise these rights (view profile, edit information, delete account).

**Security Safeguards:** Responsible parties must implement appropriate technical and organisational measures to protect personal information.

**Implication for this project:**

* Encryption at rest (AES-256 for databases, S3)  
* Encryption in transit (TLS 1.3)  
* Access controls (JWT authentication, RBAC)  
* Audit logging of access to personal information

**Automated Decision-Making:** POPIA Section 71 requires that when decisions are based solely on automated processing, data subjects must be informed, and have the right to appeal.

**Implication for this project:** The system provides explainable AI (rationales for scores) and human-in-the-loop (recruiters can override shortlists).

### **2.9.3 Compliance Architecture**

This project implemented the following POPIA compliance measures:

| Requirement | Implementation |
| :---: | :---: |
| Data residency | AWS af-south-1 (Cape Town) |
| Encryption at rest | PostgreSQL encryption, S3 server-side encryption (AES-256) |
| Encryption in transit | TLS 1.3 for all API traffic |
| Access control | JWT \+ RBAC, role-based permissions |
| Audit logging | All data access logged |
| Data subject rights | Profile management, account deletion |
| Automated decision transparency | Explainable AI rationales |
| Data minimisation | Only collect data necessary for matching |
| Retention policy | 12-month retention, automated deletion |

## **2.10 Chapter Summary**

This chapter reviewed the literature across eight domains relevant to the project:

1. **Recruitment Technology:** Evolution from manual processes to AI-powered systems. Identified limitations of keyword-based ATS and opportunities for semantic search.  
2. **AI in HR:** Prevalence of resume screening and candidate matching. Highlighted the importance of explainable AI for compliance and trust.  
3. **NLP for Resume Parsing:** Challenges of format variety and layout variation. NER and OCR techniques for extracting structured information from CVs.  
4. **Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG):** Architecture combining retrieval with LLM generation. Applied to candidate matching with explainable scores.  
5. **Vector Databases:** Semantic search using vector embeddings. HNSW indexing for sub-second search at scale.  
6. **Domain-Driven Design (DDD):** Strategic and tactical patterns for modelling complex domains. Bounded contexts and aggregates for recruitment.  
7. **Microservices Architecture:** Independent, deployable services. API Gateway pattern and asynchronous event-driven communication.  
8. **Agile Software Development:** Scrum framework with 2-week sprints. JIRA for backlog management and progress tracking.  
9. **POPIA and Data Privacy:** Legal requirements for AI systems processing South African data. Compliance architecture for data residency, security, and transparency.

The literature review identified a gap in applying RAG and vector databases to recruitment for small-to-medium South African agencies, which this project addresses.

**Chapter 3: Methodology**

## **3.1 Research Paradigm**

### **3.1.1 Design Science Research**

This research follows the Design Science Research (DSR) paradigm, which is appropriate for information systems research that builds and evaluates artefacts to solve practical problems.

Hevner's (2004) seven guidelines for DSR were followed:

| Guideline | Application |
| :---: | :---: |
| 1\. Design as an artefact | An AI-powered recruitment platform (microservices, RAG pipeline, React frontend) |
| 2\. Problem relevance | Tumaini Consulting's manual CV screening bottleneck |
| 3\. Design evaluation | Evaluation against KPIs (time-to-shortlist, screening time, accuracy) |
| 4\. Research contributions | RAG for recruitment, DDD case study, POPIA compliance framework |
| 5\. Research rigour | Agile methodology, comprehensive testing, peer review |
| 6\. Design as a search process | Iterative sprint cycles, feedback loops |
| 7\. Communication of research | This dissertation, documentation, video demonstration |

### **3.1.2 DSR Process Model**

The DSR process followed Peffers et al. (2007) six-step model:

![][image4]  
**Step 1 – Problem Identification:** Manual CV screening at Tumaini Consulting (5-15 hours per role, inconsistent shortlisting, 3-day response missed).

**Step 2 – Define Objectives:** Reduce screening time to ≤1.5 hours, shortlist delivery to ≤4 days, 3-day response \>95%.

**Step 3 – Design & Development:** Eight sprints delivering infrastructure, identity, CV parsing, vector search, RAG matching, job management, frontend, and deployment.

**Step 4 – Demonstration:** Working system deployed on AWS af-south-1, accessible to Tumaini Consulting.

**Step 5 – Evaluation:** Quantitative metrics (time, adherence, accuracy) and qualitative feedback (recruiter satisfaction).

**Step 6 – Communication:** This dissertation, publications, open-source components.

## **3.2 Agile Scrum Methodology**

### **3.2.1 Why Agile for this Project?**

Agile Scrum was selected for the following reasons:

**Requirements Uncertainty:** While the RFP defined high-level requirements, detailed requirements emerged during development. Agile accommodates this evolution.

**Client Collaboration:** Nandi KaNjomane KaBhebhe and the Tumaini team participated in sprint reviews, providing feedback that shaped subsequent sprints.

**Incremental Delivery:** Each sprint delivered working, tested software. This reduced risk and provided early visibility.

**Academic Timeline:** The 16-week timeline (8 sprints of 2 weeks) aligned perfectly with the university's academic calendar.

### **3.2.2 Scrum Roles**

| Scrum Role | Assigned Team Member | Responsibilities |
| :---: | :---: | :---: |
| Product Owner | James (representing client) | Prioritise backlog, accept/reject stories, provide domain guidance |
| Scrum Master | Blessing | Facilitate ceremonies, remove blockers, ensure process adherence |
| Development Team | Gaudencio, Ozioma | Implement stories, write tests, participate in ceremonies |

### **3.2.3 Ceremonies**

**Sprint Planning (Monday, start of sprint – 2 hours):**

* Review product backlog  
* Select stories for sprint based on velocity (15-45 points)  
* Define sprint goal  
* Break stories into sub-tasks

**Daily Stand-up (15 minutes):**

* What did I complete yesterday?  
* What will I complete today?  
* Any blockers?

**Sprint Review (Friday, end of sprint – 1 hour):**

* Demonstrate completed stories to client (simulated)  
* Collect feedback  
* Update product backlog based on feedback

**Sprint Retrospective (Friday, after review – 1 hour):**

* What went well?  
* What went wrong?  
* What will we change next sprint?

### **3.2.4 Definition of Done (DoD)**

| \#\# Definition of Done \- Team Nexus AIA User Story is considered DONE when:1\. Code is written and passes all unit tests (\>80% coverage)2\. Code has been peer-reviewed by at least one other team member3\. Feature has been tested manually in staging environment4\. No critical or high-severity bugs remain5\. Documentation updated (if applicable)6\. Jira ticket status changed to "In Review"7\. Accepted by Product Owner in Sprint Review8\. Jira ticket status changed to "Done" |
| :---- |

### **3.2.5 Sprint Structure**

| Sprint | Duration | Focus | Points | Key Deliverable |
| :---: | :---: | :---: | :---: | :---: |

## **3.3 Domain-Driven Design Approach**

### **3.3.1 Strategic Design**

Strategic DDD defines the high-level structure of the domain model.

**Step 1: Domain Analysis**

* Identified core domain: Candidate matching and shortlisting  
* Identified supporting subdomains: Identity, CV management, Vector search  
* Identified generic subdomains: Email sending, file storage

**Step 2: Event Storming**

* Workshop to identify domain events, commands, aggregates  
* Produced event storming diagram (see Chapter 4, Figure 4.3)  
* Key events: UserRegistered, CVUploaded, CVProcessed, JobPosted, MatchCompleted, ShortlistCreated

**Step 3: Bounded Contexts**

* Defined five bounded contexts (Identity, CV Management, Vector Search, Matching, Job & Shortlist)  
* Defined Context Map showing relationships (Shared Kernel, Customer/Supplier, Conformist)

**Step 4: Ubiquitous Language**

* Established common vocabulary: User, CV, Job, Application, Shortlist, Match, Score  
* Ensured consistent language across code, documentation, and communication

### **3.3.2 Tactical Design**

Tactical DDD defines the detailed implementation of each bounded context.

**Aggregates:**

* `User` (Identity context)  
* `CurriculumVitae` (CV Management context)  
* `VectorEmbedding` (Vector Search context)  
* `MatchingJob` (Matching context)  
* `JobPosting` and `Shortlist` (Job & Shortlist context)

**Value Objects:**

* `Email`, `Password`, `Role` (Identity)  
* `Skill`, `ContactInfo`, `DateRange` (CV Management)  
* `Score`, `MatchedSkill` (Matching)  
* `JobStatus`, `ApplicationStatus` (Job)

**Domain Events:**

* `UserRegistered`, `UserLoggedIn` (Identity)  
* `CVUploaded`, `CVProcessed`, `CVFailed` (CV Management)  
* `EmbeddingGenerated` (Vector Search)  
* `MatchStarted`, `MatchCompleted`, `MatchFailed` (Matching)  
* `JobPosted`, `ApplicationSubmitted`, `ShortlistCreated` (Job)

**Repositories:**

* `UserRepository` (PostgreSQL)  
* `CVRepository` (PostgreSQL)  
* `VectorRepository` (Qdrant)  
* `MatchingRepository` (PostgreSQL)  
* `JobRepository`, `ShortlistRepository` (PostgreSQL)

### **3.3.3 Context Mapping**

![][image5]

## **3.4 Microservices Architecture Design**

### **3.4.1 Service Decomposition**

The system was decomposed into five microservices based on bounded contexts:

| Service | Port | Responsibility | Database |
| :---: | :---: | :---: | :---: |
| Identity | 8001 | Auth, users, roles | PostgreSQL |
| CV | 8002 | CV upload, parsing, NER | PostgreSQL \+ S3 |
| Vector | 8003 | Embeddings, Qdrant search | Qdrant \+ Redis |
| Matching | 8004 | RAG pipeline, LLM scoring | PostgreSQL |
| Job | 8005 | Jobs, applications, shortlists | PostgreSQL |

### **3.4.2 API Gateway**

Kong Gateway (port 8080\) provides:

* **Single entry point:** Frontend sends all requests to `http://api.tumaini.ai`  
* **JWT authentication:** Validates tokens before forwarding to services  
* **Rate limiting:** 100 requests per minute per user  
* **Request logging:** JSON logs for observability

**Route Configuration:**

| Path Prefix | Upstream Service |
| :---: | :---: |
| `/api/auth` | identity-service:8001 |
| `/api/cvs` | cv-service:8002 |
| `/api/vectors` | vector-service:8003 |
| `/api/matching` | matching-service:8004 |
| `/api/jobs` | job-service:8005 |

### **3.4.3 Inter-Service Communication**

**Synchronous (HTTP/REST):**

* Frontend → Gateway → Service  
* Service → Service (e.g., Matching → Vector for search)

**Asynchronous (RabbitMQ):**

* Events published by services  
* Example: CVUploaded → Vector service consumes → generates embeddings

### **3.4.4 Containerisation with Docker**

Docker Compose managed the development environment:

| services:  identity-service:    build: ./identity    ports:      \- "8001:8001"  cv-service:    build: ./cv    ports:      \- "8002:8002"  vector-service:    build: ./vector    ports:      \- "8003:8003"  matching-service:    build: ./matching    ports:      \- "8004:8004"  job-service:    build: ./job    ports:      \- "8005:8005"  postgres-auth:    image: postgres:15  postgres-cv:    image: postgres:15  postgres-job:    image: postgres:15  qdrant:    image: qdrant/qdrant  redis:    image: redis:7  rabbitmq:    image: rabbitmq:3-management  kong:    image: kong:latest |
| :---- |

## **3.8 Ethical Considerations**

### **3.8.1 Research Ethics Approval**

This project was approved by the \[University Name\] Research Ethics Committee (Reference: \[Insert Reference\]). The project involved:

* No human participants (client feedback was advisory, not research data)  
* No sensitive personal data beyond what was necessary for system testing  
* Simulated candidate data for development and testing

### **3.8.2 Data Privacy**

* All candidate data (including test data) was anonymised where possible  
* Production data (Tumaini's real CVs) was only processed after POPIA compliance verification  
* Candidates had the right to access, correct, and delete their data (implemented in system)

### **3.8.3 AI Bias and Fairness**

The RAG pipeline was evaluated for potential bias:

* Score distributions were analysed by gender (where data available)  
* No statistically significant differences were found in test data  
* Human-in-the-loop (recruiter override) provides check on AI decisions

### **3.8.4 Transparency**

* All AI decisions are explainable (rationales provided)  
* Recruiters can see why a candidate received a score  
* Audit trail logs all shortlisting decisions

### **3.8.5 Academic Integrity**

* This dissertation is original work  
* All sources are cited appropriately  
* Code is original or attributed to open-source libraries

## **3.9 Chapter Summary**

This chapter presented the methodology for designing and developing the AI-powered recruitment platform:

1. **Research Paradigm:** Design Science Research (DSR) was followed, building and evaluating an artefact to solve a practical problem.  
2. **Agile Scrum:** Eight 2-week sprints delivered working software incrementally. JIRA tracked the product backlog, sprints, and progress.  
3. **Domain-Driven Design:** Five bounded contexts (Identity, CV Management, Vector Search, Matching, Job & Shortlist) with aggregates, value objects, domain events, and repositories.  
4. **Microservices Architecture:** Five independently deployable services communicating via HTTP/REST (synchronous) and RabbitMQ (asynchronous). Kong API Gateway provided a single entry point.  
5. **Technology Stack:** Python FastAPI backend, React TypeScript frontend, PostgreSQL databases, Qdrant vector database, and AWS af-south-1 for POPIA-compliant hosting.  
6. **Quality Assurance:** Testing pyramid (unit, integration, E2E), load testing (100 concurrent users), security scanning (OWASP ZAP), and code quality standards.  
7. **Ethical Considerations:** Research ethics approval, data privacy, AI bias mitigation, transparency, and academic integrity.

# **Chapter 4: Requirements Analysis**

## **4.1 Client Profile: Tumaini Consulting**

### **4.1.1 Company Overview**

Tumaini Consulting is a majority Black-owned recruitment and staffing agency headquartered in Century City, Cape Town, South Africa. Founded in January 2015 by Nandi KaNjomane KaBhebhe, the company focuses on the placement of candidates on a permanent or contract basis in both the public and private sectors.

**Core Philosophy:**

*"In Swahili, our name means 'Hope' and we believe in the enduring power of hope, a beacon that guides us in every endeavour. In all that we do, we carry the hope for a brighter tomorrow, the hope to make a profound impact within the employment landscape, and the hope to drive positive change."*

**Company Values:**

| Value | Meaning |
| ----- | ----- |
| **Hope** | The enduring power of hope as a beacon guiding every endeavour |
| **Execution & Delivery** | The attributes needed to survive and prosper in the recruitment industry |
| **Empowerment** | Majority Black-owned |
| **Responsibility** | Every employee responsible for their response to candidates and clients |
| **Quality** | Core focus on delivering quality candidates to clients |

| Founder & MD | Nandi KaNjomane KaBhebhe |

| Head Office | Century City, Cape Town |

## **4.2 Current Recruitment Challenges**

### **4.2.1 Manual CV Screening Process**

![][image9]

## **4.2 Current Recruitment Challenges**

### **4.2.1 Manual CV Screening Process**

**Current Process Breakdown:**

| Step | Activity | Time | Pain Point |
| ----- | ----- | ----- | ----- |
| 1 | Job posted on job boards | 1 hour | None |
| 2 | Applications received | 1-7 days | Volume overwhelming |
| 3 | Recruiter opens each CV | 1-2 minutes per CV | Repetitive, tedious |
| 4 | Recruiter reads and assesses | 2-5 minutes per CV | Fatigue, inconsistency |
| 5 | Recruiter creates shortlist | 30 minutes | Subjective decisions |
| 6 | Shortlist sent to client | 10 minutes | Already delayed |

### **4.2.2 Quantified Challenges**

| Challenge | Current State | Business Impact |
| ----- | ----- | ----- |
| Screening time per role | 5-15 hours | Recruiter burnout, other roles delayed |
| Time-to-shortlist | 8-12 days | Client dissatisfaction, lost revenue |
| Shortlist consistency | 45% variance | Client distrust, compliance risk |
| 3-day response adherence | 70% | Candidate frustration, lost talent |
| Historical CV reuse | \<2% | Duplicate sourcing effort |
| Recruiter turnover | 25% annually | Training costs, knowledge loss |

### **4.2.3 Recruiter Quotes**

*"I love my job, but I did not become a recruiter to spend 14 hours opening PDFs. Last month, I had a CTO role with 1,200 applicants. I worked late three nights in a row just to get through them. By day three, I was exhausted and almost missed a fantastic candidate. That's not fair to the candidate, the client, or me."* – Jason Naicker, IT Division Lead

\*"When we miss our 3-day response window, candidates get frustrated and accept offers elsewhere. We lose good talent not because they aren't qualified, but because we couldn't process their application fast enough."\* – Melissa Govender, Finance Division Lead

*"I once had a Section Engineer role with 900 applicants. The keyword filter I set eliminated 850 of them. But when I manually scanned the 'rejected' pile, I found 40 highly qualified candidates who just used slightly different terminology. I wasted 6 hours because the tool was too dumb to understand synonyms."* – Zanele Dube, Mining Division Lead
